    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer3280444072053190723"><i>Freedom or Death</i> by Nikos Kazantzakis is a novel on the heroic or epic scale about the rebellion of the Greek Christians against the Turks on the island of Crete, where Kazantzakis was from. The story follows the exploits of a Greek: Captain Michalis and his blood brother, Nurey Bey, a Turk, through war, love , friendship, hatred and a backdrop of the island of Crete with </span>
  <span id="freeText3280444072053190723" style="display:none"><i>Freedom or Death</i> by Nikos Kazantzakis is a novel on the heroic or epic scale about the rebellion of the Greek Christians against the Turks on the island of Crete, where Kazantzakis was from. The story follows the exploits of a Greek: Captain Michalis and his blood brother, Nurey Bey, a Turk, through war, love , friendship, hatred and a backdrop of the island of Crete with all its beauty, drama, joy and sadness. This book was unanimously praised by critics worldwide as the work of a master with characters that come to life and destined to live forever.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12446542077682879768">Ο καπετάν Μιχάλης = Captain Michalis = Freedom or Death, Nikos Kazantzakis <br/>Captain Michalis is a 1953 novel by the Greek writer Nikos Kazantzakis. It is known as Freedom and Death in the United Kingdom. The book deals with the rebellion of the Cretans against the Ottoman Empire in 1889. It is thought that the book's title honors Kazantzakis' father Michalis Kazantzakis, by whom the writer was inspired. The word Captain is not used in its naval rank sense, but as the title of leader of Guerilla g</span>
  <span id="freeText12446542077682879768" style="display:none">Ο καπετάν Μιχάλης = Captain Michalis = Freedom or Death, Nikos Kazantzakis <br />Captain Michalis is a 1953 novel by the Greek writer Nikos Kazantzakis. It is known as Freedom and Death in the United Kingdom. The book deals with the rebellion of the Cretans against the Ottoman Empire in 1889. It is thought that the book's title honors Kazantzakis' father Michalis Kazantzakis, by whom the writer was inspired. The word Captain is not used in its naval rank sense, but as the title of leader of Guerilla group (the writer's father Michalis Kazantzakis was a leader of such a group, hence the title. Kazantzakis says this in his book "Report to Greco"). Freedom or Death was added as a subtitle to the second edition in Greek released by Difros publishers in Athens in 1955 and was the preferred English (US) title. In the UK the book was published as Freedom and Death, which were the last words in the book<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: بیست و نهم ماه اکتبر سال 2009 میلادی<br />عنوان: آزادی یا مرگ؛ نویسنده: نیکوس کازانتزاکیس؛ مترجم: محمد قاضی؛ تهران، خوارزمی، 1362؛ در 730 ص؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان یونانی - سده 20 م<br /><br />رمانی از «نیکوس کازانتزاکیس»، نویسنده یونانی، که در سال 1950 میلادی با عنوان اصلی «کاپیتان میخالیس» نوشته و در سال 1953 میلادی در «آتن» منتشر شد. قهرمان اصلی کاپیتان یا پهلوان میخالیس، از بسیاری جهات پدر خود نویسنده را به یاد می‌آورد، خیالبافی افسانه‌ وار و سرگذشت واقعی پی در پی به هم می‌آمیزند. نویسنده در فصلهای نخست کتاب دیگرش، با عنوان: «نامه به ال گرکو» نیز، چهره این مرد عبوس، ساکت، خشن و سازش‌ ناپذیر را، که حتی ترس و وحشت را در میان خانواده خود، حکمفرما می‌ساخت، مجسم کرده است. در این کتاب «آزادی یا مرگ» که در آن داستان دو شورش «جزیره کِرت» را، در سالهای 1889 میلادی و سال 1897 میلادی، بر ضد ترکان «عثمانی»، یکجا درهم آمیخته است، پهلوان «میخالیس» به عنوان مظهر شورش و قیام، در برابر آنچه انسان را زجر داده، و زندگی او را به صورت سرنوشتی ساده، و بی‌اهمیت درمیآورد؛ نمودار می‌شود. در این داستان، نیروی اشغالگر، زن و حتی خانواده، هرکدام مانع آدمی، در راه نیل به آزادی هستند. از اینرو، پس از کشتاری که ترکان از مسیحیان شهر «کاندی»، در «کِرت» می‌کنند، پهلوان «میخالیس»، یکی از نخستین کسانی است، که راه مبارزه ی مخفی را، در پیش می‌گیرد، و آخرین کسی است، که در آنجا، با رد هرگونه سازش، و هرنوع تمکین، جان می‌سپارد. او در آخرین مأمنی که در آن پناه گرفته است، در حالیکه سلاح به دست دارد، و گروهی از فداییان دور و برش هستند، کشته می‌شود. در اطراف این قهرمان، جمعی از «کرتیان»، مرکّب از جنگجویان و مبارزان شرکت کننده در شورشهای مختلف، راهزنان، دزدان دریایی، چوپانان، دهقانان و رؤسای خانواده هستند، که همگی حقیقتی ساده و کلی را، شعار خود کرده‌ اند، و آن این است: «به هیچ قیمتی نباید به آنچه شأن و شرف آدمی را پایین می‌آورد تسلیم شد، بلکه باید مبارزه کرد و برای آزادی زنده بود و مُرد». در این جهان که آداب و رسوم کهن بر آن حکمفرماست، بزرگمردی همواره دوش به دوش شجاعت روان است. «سیفاکاس» پیر، پدربزرگ پهلوان «میخالیس»، مظهر آن دنیای کهن است. مردی است با فضایل و ملکات استوار، که در او جوانمردی، گویی جزء جدایی‌ناپذیر خشونت، و شجاعت است، کتاب، برای نویسنده بازگشتی به سالهای کودکی است، در مورد شورش «کرتیان» بر «ترکان عثمانی»، بیان‌ کننده ی حکمتی است، که نویسنده پیشتر در سال 1927 میلادی، در نخستین اثرش با عنوان «ریاضت» وصف کرده بود، و آن این است: «فضیلت مبارزه کردن برای آزادی والاست، چندان که خود آزاد بودن نیست.»؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="12446542077682879768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1637090137" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1637090137" />
    <a title="Beatrice Apetrei" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22548668-beatrice-apetrei"><img alt="Beatrice Apetrei" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586449703p2/22548668.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1637090137?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beatrice Apetrei" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beatrice Apetrei" href="/user/show/22548668-beatrice-apetrei">Beatrice Apetrei</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548668-beatrice-apetrei?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548668-beatrice-apetrei?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1637090137" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4879966453774152606">Freedom or Death is the fourth book written by Greek author Nikos Kazantzakis that I managed to get my hands on. I started it not because of the summary or plot, or good reviews, but because of its author ( and I have no shame to admit it ). Of course, it doesn’t mean there were no chances of disliking it, that was one of the possibilities, but him being my favorite author so far and having read other 3 books by him in the last 6 months, I was somehow certain of it.<br/><br/>Reading his previous works, I </span>
  <span id="freeText4879966453774152606" style="display:none">Freedom or Death is the fourth book written by Greek author Nikos Kazantzakis that I managed to get my hands on. I started it not because of the summary or plot, or good reviews, but because of its author ( and I have no shame to admit it ). Of course, it doesn’t mean there were no chances of disliking it, that was one of the possibilities, but him being my favorite author so far and having read other 3 books by him in the last 6 months, I was somehow certain of it.<br /><br />Reading his previous works, I was always intrigued by his characters never-dying desire to see Crete released of the Turkish authority. For Kazantzakis, Crete is not only his physical birth place, but his <i>souls</i> home. No matter where he traveled, what he saw and learned, where he lived, his mind was always in Crete and his heart always bled for it. Don’t get me wrong, I don’t know much history and all I know about the ottoman dominion there is from his books only. <br />In my opinion though, this obsession of his with Crete is exhausting. Most of his <b>protagonists</b> are exhausting. Beautiful, but exhausting. The whole time I was reading this, I kept wondering, what makes them so passionate about a freedom that shows itself only when death occurs ? Why can’t they see that there is more to life than this controlling obsession they allowed to take root in their minds ? <br /><br />There was this fearless power that tied strong men to Crete, making them loyal to <i>it</i>. A power that makes men deny themselves any kind of pleasure because they don’t find it appropriate to laugh or smile while having Crete under the Turkish rule. Here, Crete is a living being, breathing, screaming and at night going silent. It is observed by many and pitied for its helpless desire to be free. But it keeps fighting, men keep fighting.<br />It’s a story about loyalty, hatred, pride, reason, death, ambition and freedom. Or is it better said <i> lack of freedom </i> ? Lack of freedom drives men insane, discourages them, holds them prisoners. <br /><br />How does this affect their lives ? The connection they have with other people ( children, wives ), their feelings, desires ? That’s what this novel is about : the lives of those who had fallen under the charms of this dream. <br /><br />I have talked about Kazantzakis’ <b> writing </b> in a previously written review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82531.Christ_Recrucified" title="Christ Recrucified by Nikos Kazantzakis" rel="nofollow noopener">Christ Recrucified</a>, I will not repeat myself, just add that somehow <i> it </i> manages to express how events, places, spaces, people are perceived in a way that makes everything feel horrifyingly <i>real</i>. <br />I don’t believe this novel is for everyone, not because it’s hard to read, but, how I’ve previously stated, it can easily exhaust you. It made me sigh a lot, made me sad and left me with a bitter taste. After all, this book is about freedom <b>and</b> death. <br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4879966453774152606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17856531760694639996">Being Turkish, of course i read this book nervously. All the Turkish characters in the book are depicted scornfully. Weak, homosexual, coward, cruel, whatever you like. The only character Kazancakis speaks highly of is Nuri Bey. But then he pimps his wife to our great hero, almost animal, captain Mihalis. <br/><br/>Anyway, this book will lead me to a serious history reading of that period. Ottoman empire did not conquer those lands by giving flowers, i know that. But even in this nonobjective book i can s</span>
  <span id="freeText17856531760694639996" style="display:none">Being Turkish, of course i read this book nervously. All the Turkish characters in the book are depicted scornfully. Weak, homosexual, coward, cruel, whatever you like. The only character Kazancakis speaks highly of is Nuri Bey. But then he pimps his wife to our great hero, almost animal, captain Mihalis. <br /><br />Anyway, this book will lead me to a serious history reading of that period. Ottoman empire did not conquer those lands by giving flowers, i know that. But even in this nonobjective book i can see greek people of that island had their religous freedom. They were free to merchandise, get rich, do whatever they like. Only the rulers were Ottoman officials. So Ottoman occupation should not be compared to English occupation of India for example. <br /><br />Well, a little nervous by nationalism, i may not be objective either. <br /><br />Another thing which disturbed me while reading this book is Kazancakis's huge praise for machismo. If i were a woman reading this book, i would tear it maybe. Women are worthless slaves in Girit and this is so beatiful, so heroic, isn't it? Kazancakis praises uncivilization, praises barbarism and despises the beatiful scents of Turkish homes. Kazancakis admires brute force. Keeping hatred alive between nations is easer than seeing each others good sides. <br /><br />But if put my feelings aside, this a modern classic of course. A good example of epic. Terrible ideology put in a nice form. Perfect craftmanship.<br /><br />This may give more light on Crete: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretan_Turks">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretan_T...</a> <br /><br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/243042884?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ensiform" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ensiform" href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform">Ensiform</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer243042884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13226038653185548037">Translated by Jonathan Griffin. This 500-page monster is another novel, set during the 1889 rebellion in Crete, soaked in the joy of living, rife with graphic fighting, killing, sex, feasting, drinking, and deep, calm spirituality. Kazantzakis' obsession with the duality of human nature is everywhere apparent, for example in the opposition of the wild Captain Michales to his blood brother and sworn enemy, the pleasure-loving Turk Nuri Bey; in some minor characters' attempt to reconcile sainthood</span>
  <span id="freeText13226038653185548037" style="display:none">Translated by Jonathan Griffin. This 500-page monster is another novel, set during the 1889 rebellion in Crete, soaked in the joy of living, rife with graphic fighting, killing, sex, feasting, drinking, and deep, calm spirituality. Kazantzakis' obsession with the duality of human nature is everywhere apparent, for example in the opposition of the wild Captain Michales to his blood brother and sworn enemy, the pleasure-loving Turk Nuri Bey; in some minor characters' attempt to reconcile sainthood and enjoyment of the earth; in the lusty old grandfather's final question concerning the meaning of life; the juxtaposition of the very old and the newly adult, the dead and the fortunately alive. Many scenes are quite memorable, showing a bloody knife fight or men who get drunk and jump for the joy of life over the corpse they are supposed to be watching. The final scene of death and sacrifice is the most powerful. It's a great book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13226038653185548037" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour"><img alt="J.M. Hushour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442964894p2/17463213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.M. Hushour" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour">J.M. Hushour</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3044270077" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4861624415863444536">"Sins only began to bring real satisfaction when one was well and truly up to the neck in them."<br/><br/>Kazantzakis, one of those genuine prose masters of the last century and one of the deepest sounders of the human soul, uses the backdrop of a failed 1889 Cretan uprising against its Ottoman rules as a backdrop against which to explore the fouler, bestial angles of our nature.<br/>K. was a child when this revolt occurred and his father, a certain Captain Michales (the Greek title of this novel), was apparen</span>
  <span id="freeText4861624415863444536" style="display:none">"Sins only began to bring real satisfaction when one was well and truly up to the neck in them."<br /><br />Kazantzakis, one of those genuine prose masters of the last century and one of the deepest sounders of the human soul, uses the backdrop of a failed 1889 Cretan uprising against its Ottoman rules as a backdrop against which to explore the fouler, bestial angles of our nature.<br />K. was a child when this revolt occurred and his father, a certain Captain Michales (the Greek title of this novel), was apparently active in the fighting, so we can assume the author used a hefty dollop of reality in this often disturbing local folk tale. I say 'folk tale' because despite the heavy political and historical overtones, they never overwhelm the characters, who are all well-cared for here. The novel is essentially a kind of mythological exploration of how awful people can be and how the people enduring the awful people deal with it.<br />Captain Michales is the central character here, and like K's Christ is flawed yet vehement. He forces other villagers into his basement for 6-day benders in holy weeks, taunts his blood-brother the Ottoman Nuri Bey and secretly lusts after the latter's wife.<br />But Crete supplants all in his affections and when rebellion breaks out yet again, he heads for the hills with various brothers, sons, and nephews to harry the pasha's forces and murder people for no good reason.<br />One of the things that stands out here, and this will sit well with anyone familiar with the welcome tropes of current arts, is that no one here is a very good person. The bystanders are good people, almost all women or the humbly religious, but the fiery partisans and their Ottoman counterparts, surprisingly often their friends, are demonic beasts who refuse to lay down their arms. As the fighting escalates and the inner turmoil of the characters rises to the fore, K. makes some nice shifts of perspective, from the deathbed denizen, the Captain's father, to the recently-returned nephew of Michales who has been in mainland Europe for years and married a Jewish woman.<br />Troubling and poetic!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4861624415863444536" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_106166784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106166784" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3333943-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267037102p2/3333943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/106166784?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/3333943-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571178575" href="/book/show/204023.Freedom_and_Death">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of historical fiction, translated lit</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=20th-century-fiction">20th-century-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=books-from-1956">books-from-1956</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer106166784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2779184419650333987"><br/> In his third novel, Kazantzakis turns to his birthplace. He was born in Crete in 1885 and in 1897, Greek Christians took up arms to rebel against the Turkish conquerors who had ruled Crete since the mid 1600s. This novel is the story of that rebellion. In my reading of The Life of Greece, The Story of Civilization, Volume II by Will Durant, I learned that Crete was the center of Europe's most ancient civilization: the Minoan, from which we got the legends of King Minos, the Minotaur, Daedalus a</span>
  <span id="freeText2779184419650333987" style="display:none"><br /> In his third novel, Kazantzakis turns to his birthplace. He was born in Crete in 1885 and in 1897, Greek Christians took up arms to rebel against the Turkish conquerors who had ruled Crete since the mid 1600s. This novel is the story of that rebellion. In my reading of The Life of Greece, The Story of Civilization, Volume II by Will Durant, I learned that Crete was the center of Europe's most ancient civilization: the Minoan, from which we got the legends of King Minos, the Minotaur, Daedalus and Icarus.<br /><br /> Reading Kazantzakis is challenging for me. In all three novels, he spends many pages introducing characters and describing the surroundings without much story going on. Because of unfamiliar names, I started a list of characters and locations. I was up to 26 found on just the first 40 pages. But what characters they are! Full of life and exhibiting all the strengths and faults of human beings, no matter which side they are on.<br /><br /> Once the story of the rebellion gets going, it is as gripping as any of the best historical fiction I have read. His themes of freedom, religion and the fully lived life are explored against the backdrop of the oppressions, honor and passion involved in any rebellion. And the ultimate question of whether it is better to die for freedom than to live under oppression gets told from another location in this crazy world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2779184419650333987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16888424290219335015">The first half of this book is excellent. Kazantzakis portrays Greek life under Turkish rule so well that I could feel and smell and see parts of the village. His characters act with vitality and motivation that feels true.   <span id="freeText16888424290219335015" style="display:none">The first half of this book is excellent. Kazantzakis portrays Greek life under Turkish rule so well that I could feel and smell and see parts of the village. His characters act with vitality and motivation that feels true. One nocturnal scene in which the village women collectively feel the carnal urge while the men sleep uneasily in the heat will stay with me for life.<br /><br />In the second half of the book, Kazantzakis moves onto actual conflict. Events happen at a more hectic pace, and the pastoral setting is replaced by mountainous terrain fit for ambushes and hideouts. The tight control that Kazantzakis held over the first part of the book slips until a very final ending that surprised me.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16242585763771339983">This book has been called Kazantzakis's "modern Iliad." The writing and emotion are epic, but it is, at its heart, a deeply personal story about Crete, homeland, and freedom. It also explores some of Kazantzakis's favorite themes such as temptation, love, sacrifice, and enlightenment. How the readers feels will also be personal--the details of Crete will either captivate or bore, the philosophy will either intrigue or frustrate. It remains a major work of art from one of the great authors of the</span>
  <span id="freeText16242585763771339983" style="display:none">This book has been called Kazantzakis's "modern Iliad." The writing and emotion are epic, but it is, at its heart, a deeply personal story about Crete, homeland, and freedom. It also explores some of Kazantzakis's favorite themes such as temptation, love, sacrifice, and enlightenment. How the readers feels will also be personal--the details of Crete will either captivate or bore, the philosophy will either intrigue or frustrate. It remains a major work of art from one of the great authors of the twentieth century. Definitely worth reading.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571178575" href="/book/show/204023.Freedom_and_Death">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer100291579&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100291579" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12070699829522486877">I enjoyed the book, although not quite as thought-provoking as I expected. <br/><br/>The title itself is of some interest because throughout the book, when the phrase is repeated by characters, the conjunction is “Or”, not the title’s “And”. The plot, however, features endless uprisings, repeated and fruitless bloodshed, dumb pride and little to no gains. <br/><br/>The protagonist is a great masculine, warrior type of romantic figure. A simple but a true hero but there are hints of underlying passions. He verbally </span>
  <span id="freeText12070699829522486877" style="display:none">I enjoyed the book, although not quite as thought-provoking as I expected. <br /><br />The title itself is of some interest because throughout the book, when the phrase is repeated by characters, the conjunction is “Or”, not the title’s “And”. The plot, however, features endless uprisings, repeated and fruitless bloodshed, dumb pride and little to no gains. <br /><br />The protagonist is a great masculine, warrior type of romantic figure. A simple but a true hero but there are hints of underlying passions. He verbally claims to hate a woman, but his actions, including the hatred, are too passionate. Hatred is not indifference. His tragic story may be the romantic figure of all of Crete.<br /><br /><br />The book does paint a wonderful picture of Crete. Like a constantly moving camera lens which focuses on new family to new family, adding their quick family story. Through the method, one gets to learn the village as I think a long time resident does. This is done so well that it makes Crete seem like such a beautiful place that anyone could have effectively described it. (I haven’t visited but I will bet it is very much a beautiful place, but his artistic ability to convey this beauty shouldn't be diminished for it. Credit to the translator, too)<br /><br />Relationships between couples are often discussed and one learns the culture and people this way. While people claim to have a conservative and reserved sensitivity, passions run hot. Lots of male and female promiscuity. For one memorable couple, the wife regularly beats the husband who wails in pain so loudly that neighbors hear it nightly. When men in the town confront the husband and beseech him to be a man, he confesses "bur I enjoying it...”. In the types of stories, I think we get the feel for a close knit community where people know each other a lot better than I know my NYC neighbors.<br /><br />Overall, Freedom and Death is a simple but enjoyable read. I have seen it billed as “philosophy” but it’s not a head scratcher or challengingly deep. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12070699829522486877" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_276710308" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/276710308" />
    <a title="Kostas D." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7729995-kostas-d"><img alt="Kostas D." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329101327p2/7729995.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/276710308?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kostas D." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kostas D." href="/user/show/7729995-kostas-d">Kostas D.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer276710308" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13667377725185712569">‘Freedom and Death’ is an intense novel. Moreover, it’s a source informing us about the way Cretans used to live during the period of Turkish conquest.<br/>In Kazantzaki’s novel, just like in real life, Cretans appear to be a proud people, longing to see their Mother Land freed. In Megalokastro, were most of the story unfolds, Greeks, Turks, Armenians, and Jews live together. Turks are the local authority, of course, as a part of the Ottoman Empire. And this is where Captain Michales, a brave resista</span>
  <span id="freeText13667377725185712569" style="display:none">‘Freedom and Death’ is an intense novel. Moreover, it’s a source informing us about the way Cretans used to live during the period of Turkish conquest.<br />In Kazantzaki’s novel, just like in real life, Cretans appear to be a proud people, longing to see their Mother Land freed. In Megalokastro, were most of the story unfolds, Greeks, Turks, Armenians, and Jews live together. Turks are the local authority, of course, as a part of the Ottoman Empire. And this is where Captain Michales, a brave resistance fighter, lives with his family. Captain Michales will be challenged by his blood-brother, Nuri Bey, and soon a series of killings among Cretans and Turks will start.<br />This is how a new rising takes place in Crete. Cretans and Ottomans will fight each other till death, and this is where the reader notices what ‘homeland’ meant to Cretans at those times. Help from west, from the Great Powers, won’t come, and the Cretans are outnumbered. And in such a context unfolds the story of the characters.<br />What caught my attention was the fact that even though different in religious beliefs, Christians and Muslims live together, sometimes as friends, sometimes as enemies, sometimes even sharing their own meals. However, their differences take action in an ‘underground’ way, so that they will lead to conflict.<br />Freedom and Death has the value of a great novel, among the writings of Greek and international literature, and the value of an interesting documentary, about the way Christians lived their lives under Ottoman conquest. How brave they were, how they believed in their tradition and culture. Those great subjects all exist in this great novel, for the reader to notice them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13667377725185712569" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer195803882" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9410909965554113563">I recently finished reading this in the original Greek. I loved Kazantzakis use of the Greek language, he is able to paint a picture with just a few words. Not quite sure how well the English translation would hold up. Although the story is set during the late 1880's on Crete, there is a timeless feel about it. It could have taken place in any country that has been occupied for a long time, with its inhabitants battling with whether they should live peaceably with the occupiers or fight against </span>
  <span id="freeText9410909965554113563" style="display:none">I recently finished reading this in the original Greek. I loved Kazantzakis use of the Greek language, he is able to paint a picture with just a few words. Not quite sure how well the English translation would hold up. Although the story is set during the late 1880's on Crete, there is a timeless feel about it. It could have taken place in any country that has been occupied for a long time, with its inhabitants battling with whether they should live peaceably with the occupiers or fight against them in an endless struggle that might go on for generations. <br /><br />I did not find it an easy novel to read and I was shocked by the ending. I loved how well Kazantzakis drew the characters, they are not one dimensional, their lust for life and the spirit that lives within them. I particularly was interested in the moral questions that arise in this work and are voiced by the characters themselves. Kazantzakis has a way of bringing the historical into perspective with the current and this is another aspect of this work that makes it a classic. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9410909965554113563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15765304-b-bb-bbbb-bbbbbbbb?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15765304-b-bb-bbbb-bbbbbbbb?shelf=clouds-1-5">clouds-1-5</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15765304-b-bb-bbbb-bbbbbbbb?shelf=stars-2-0">stars-2-0</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15765304-b-bb-bbbb-bbbbbbbb?shelf=political-social">political-social</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1959167256" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13291990787571209768">Oh, we've got so many problems here. Machismo worship, deeply gender normative, rampantly misogynistic, glorification of violence, and tons of other dubious things. <br/><br/>It's mostly interesting for the historical characterization of life and culture in Crete around the late 1880's. The story is ok, the writing solid, the characters are well depicted (and problematic). It meanders a bit at the end. Not a great book, but readable and sometimes interesting. It did make me want to learn more about Crete.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Judi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1948879-judi"><img alt="Judi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371441188p2/1948879.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer65497364" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6475981612562517395">A first-rate novel about the conflict between Turks and Greeks on the island of Crete, a passionately written and very moving account of the historical period in Crete. Kazantzakis is a writer who is able to portray both events and people very realistically. He has a wonderful mastery of the language. I highly recommend this book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3368384010" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3368384010" />
    <a title="Kokkinos" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22458965-kokkinos"><img alt="Kokkinos" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3368384010?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kokkinos" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kokkinos" href="/user/show/22458965-kokkinos">Kokkinos</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Leather Bound 9789607948205" href="/book/show/4612857">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3368384010" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14093697913287643767">The main story of this book is a Cretan uprising againist the Ottoman Empire. The first part of the book is kind of introductory to the second part, where action takes place. Numerous characters are involved, pretty much all the Cretans share one attribute, their passion for freedom, which leads them to pretty stubborn and irrational behavior. As a result, it is hard for the modern reader to find any appeal in them based on today's standards or even more identify himself with them. On the other </span>
  <span id="freeText14093697913287643767" style="display:none">The main story of this book is a Cretan uprising againist the Ottoman Empire. The first part of the book is kind of introductory to the second part, where action takes place. Numerous characters are involved, pretty much all the Cretans share one attribute, their passion for freedom, which leads them to pretty stubborn and irrational behavior. As a result, it is hard for the modern reader to find any appeal in them based on today's standards or even more identify himself with them. On the other hand, the Turkish characters are rather shallow and barely have any redeeming qualities, with the notable exception of Nuri Bey, who is arguably the most captivating character and it is a shame he had to go so soon. The depiction of the everyday life in the island and the coexistence between Greek and Turkish people seemed to me highly accurate, as did the portrayal of the landscape, making you feel like you are participating in the events and you are not just a spectator, even though at times it was somewhat overdrawn. After the pace picks up, it gets too brutal and violent for my taste, but there are still some noteworthy parts. A particular highlight for me was the conversation between Sifakas, the father of Captain Mihalis, and his partners in the past wars, which revolved around reflecting upon their lives and wondering if after all there was any meaning to them. Also, the ending is a welcome shock, despite the writer having already heavily hinted at it. Overall, 3/5.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14093697913287643767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick P." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick P." href="/user/show/69732176-nick-p">Nick P.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2069737790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9414464233927228749">Another amazing read from Kazantzakis. Having a Cretan grandfather, I really felt my blood boil with pride, passion and bravery as I journeyed into 18th century Crete. I found myself unable to escape the charm and never-passing suspense of a Cretan revolt, and found myself dreaming of a life as a Palikare.<br/><br/>At times, Freedom and Death is exuberantly humorous, while at others it is mind numbing the brutal and horrifically tragic. <br/><br/>I highly recommend this as a must read for anyone wanting a glimpse i</span>
  <span id="freeText9414464233927228749" style="display:none">Another amazing read from Kazantzakis. Having a Cretan grandfather, I really felt my blood boil with pride, passion and bravery as I journeyed into 18th century Crete. I found myself unable to escape the charm and never-passing suspense of a Cretan revolt, and found myself dreaming of a life as a Palikare.<br /><br />At times, Freedom and Death is exuberantly humorous, while at others it is mind numbing the brutal and horrifically tragic. <br /><br />I highly recommend this as a must read for anyone wanting a glimpse into the glorified wartime life of Crete.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9414464233927228749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kkw0r0t4yPuXm9HCCDEcxK4dSIfkE1o+bEQNFCegNcCAUqGaaAC13MBPFKQDEZb+/Bcnr+so8qYj2oKvsLe9/Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5668.Nikos_Kazantzakis">About Nikos Kazantzakis</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5668.Nikos_Kazantzakis"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1206405006p3/5668.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>

<a href="/author/show/5668.Nikos_Kazantzakis">Nikos Kazantzakis

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10577836693662750307">Nikos Kazantzakis (Greek: <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17734780._________________' title='Νίκος Καζαντζάκης' rel='nofollow noopener'>Νίκος Καζαντζάκης</a>
</b>) was a prolific Greek writer, whose works include essays, novels, poems, tragedies, travel books, and translations of such classics as Dante's The Divine Comedy and J.W. von Goethe's Faust. <br/><br/>Like his hero, Odysseus, Kazantzakis lived most of his artistic life outside Greece-except for the years of World War II. "I am a mariner of Odysseus with heart o</span>
  <span id="freeText10577836693662750307" style="display:none">Nikos Kazantzakis (Greek: <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17734780._________________" title="Νίκος Καζαντζάκης" rel="nofollow noopener">Νίκος Καζαντζάκης</a>
</b>) was a prolific Greek writer, whose works include essays, novels, poems, tragedies, travel books, and translations of such classics as Dante's The Divine Comedy and J.W. von Goethe's Faust. <br /><br />Like his hero, Odysseus, Kazantzakis lived most of his artistic life outside Greece-except for the years of World War II. "I am a mariner of Odysseus with heart of fire but with mind ruthless and clear," Kazantzakis wrote in TODA RABA (1934). <br /><br />Several of the author's novels deal with the history and culture of his own country, and the mystical relationship between man and God. In 1957 he lost the Nobel Prize by a single vote to the French writer Albert Camus.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10577836693662750307" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.
This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
